## 9. Chipotle Was In Trouble Then This Happened

Welcome back to Case Study with The Biz
Doc. I'm diving back into Chipotle. Now,
why do I say diving back in? Because a

to the point of the horrible E. coli
breakout. This takes you through the
recovery and the man that did it, Brian
Niccol, executing one of the greatest
turnarounds in American business and in
definitely the restaurant industry's
history. I have the whole story this
week.
Chipotle. I'm going to take you back on
a quick glimpse back here, just a little
bit of a recap, and then I'm going to
walk through the crisis, but more
importantly, what Brian Niccol did as an
operator and the lessons for you and me,
whether we're operating an $11 billion
restaurant chain or an $11 million
software factory or an $11,000 a month
digital marketing agency. It doesn't
matter. All of the lessons of operations
come to bear. In 1983 in Denver, Steve
Ells conceived and launches Chipotle. It
goes off and it starts to growing.
They're expanding and whatnot. 15 years
into it, very successful, McDonald's,
yes, that McDonald's, bought a
controlling stake. Now, what was
interesting is Steve Ells was kind of
selling Chipotle as the anti-fast food,
the anti-McDonald's. Coming in, sit
down, grab something that's more

I'll have a number six. Now, they only

McDonald's used that opportunity to
divest, which led to the expansion and
the wonder years. So, for nine years
from 2006 to 2015, this thing is a
freight train. Steve Ells is thought of
as a hero, a great operator, a founder,
but he was an expansion and growth
founder. You've heard the expression, is
that a wartime president or a peacetime
president? Well, guess what? For CEOs
and founders, there is the same. You
could be a great peacetime and growth
CEO, but when wartime comes, you really
are a little caught out and maybe you're
not as good as the growth side of you.
And it could leave the company hanging.
That's what happened. We have here as
the stock peaks 2015, everything going
great, and then all of the sudden,
October to December of 2015, the E. coli
breakout. 60 people in 14 states get
sick. They trace it all back to
Chipotle. They test the food. They test
the source. Yep, it's E. coli. They
actually close 43 restaurants, and it
was horrible. A complete deceleration in
all the business momentum. Same store
sales drops as the public trust is lost,
and they're trying to recover in '16 and
'17 cuz the crisis didn't go away
overnight. They had people like me that
backed off the brand and didn't go there
for 2 years. That's right. I didn't go

happened was front-loaded, and you

inspection going on that it would be
pretty safe to eat there. Well, guess
what? This exposed Steve Ells of not
being a wartime and recovery founder and
CEO. That doesn't mean that he didn't
have a brilliant idea. That doesn't mean
he didn't grow it. That doesn't mean
that he didn't preside over it during
the wonder years and the stock getting
to 758. But when wartime happened and we

you have to restore public trust,

didn't quite happen. In February of
2018,
Brian Niccol comes in. He had
experience, but he had experience at
Taco Bell. And some people were saying,
"Now, wait a minute. You're coming from

they had the right guy. Let's talk about
the Niccol era. You're inheriting a
brand that still has issues with public
trust. You seem to have gotten things
behind you, but now you got to find ways
to grow. You're only going to grow with
trust and with services and a customer
attitude and maybe even some

six things that he did that are all
centered around disciplined safety,
expansion, and KPIs. He started asking
questions like, "How many burritos can
be made on this line?" "I don't know. We
typically make this many." "Well, if we
adjust this, this, and this, could we
make more? Could we set a standard?
Could we measure that? Could we drive
it?" And Niccol became known as a guy
that was strong on safety, strong on
operations, but there was also some
innovation around the menu. They would
test new menu items for up to 6 months,
not 2 weeks, not 3 weeks, not a test
kitchen in one location. They would test
it. So, when it got rolled out on a

had a hit new menu item because of 6

Chipotle Rewards. By the time he left,

were on Chipotle Rewards. A simple

gave you credits and benefits for repeat
visits and repeat meals. The next thing

Now, the nickel era I went right here,
18 to 24 when he would leave. When he

would drive it to 11.3 billion. Pretty
significant, but how did he get there?
We talk about Chipotle rewards,
incentivize the people to come back.
Then, we go to Chipotle lanes. I hope I
pronounce that right. There were now
1,000 drive-thru Chipotle lanes by the

Now, that's tremendous success, and he
also extended the reach. As he's got

menu items, he says, "We can expand."
2,400 locations when he started, 3,500
locations when he departed in 24. And

together with strong operating margin,
and you're driving the revenue, the

to the point that they did a 50-to-1

He's an operator, not a COO, Chief

24%
on the announcement of Brian Niccol.

at Starbucks bringing the magic, which

So in some ways Scott Boatwright is up
against the wall because one fault that
he has isn't really a fault. You're not
Brian Niccol. Well, no, I'm not, but I'm

there's only one word for that and that

So, now let's look at the lessons for
you and me. Founders are not always
operators. As indicated, Steve Ells was
a growth and expansion, but he was not
wartime. And when wartime hit, it wasn't

was like a $98 million package say gave
Brian Niccol to come to Starbucks. And
the market cap of Starbucks on day one,
oh, they got it back. Then, operating
excellence is a discipline. This is for
you and me. Again, you don't have to be
an $11.3 billion
fast-casual restaurant. You could be a
$11 million
software developer or a $1.1 million
digital marketing agency. Operating
excellence is a discipline, but the
playbook without the enforcer is
nothing. And that's part of what the
market is looking at right now with
Scott Boatright. You have the playbook,
but are you the driver that Brian Niccol
was? Can you show us? We're waiting.
Lastly, succession can be a silent
killer or a new chapter of success.
Sometimes the heir apparent has certain
flaws or certain things that it's so
obvious that he's going to be the heir,
and the board doesn't want to go through

You're here. You know us. You're the

boards sometimes miss that opportunity.
That can happen to you, too, because

needs or is there a better choice? Maybe
it's a new chapter of success when the
successor is groomed and ready to go
like a Tim Cook. That's the story of

## 11. Culture Fixes What Management Can't

So, when you're asking about this,
culture,
the right culture, all of a sudden
you'll see a 45 go to 50 go to 55 60 65.
Now you're doing numbers you've never
done before, and guess what else happens
when that thing hits 70 75%? You know
what happens when it's 75 70 70 75%?
Guess what happens to 80% of the
problems in the company? You're no
longer solving them. The culture solves
them.
Cuz guess what? If I sit here,
the culture I have, if I sit here and
I'm bitching and whining, you know what
our culture is? I want Frank to call me
out. Based on what you told me, he's a
guy that'll call me out. I want Frank to
say, "Stop bitching, Pat.
Why are you complaining so much? All the
flipping time you're complaining to
everybody. What are you doing? Do you
want to be here or not? Don't wait for
me to say it."
I want him to say it.
Instead of saying, "Well, listen, he's
not my direct report." No, if you're a
leader in the culture, call out
respectfully. Not an [ __ ] not a
jerk, not a bully, respectfully. Then
I'm going to be like let's just say
Frank and I I really respect Frank. Even
more than I respect the CEO. Cuz you
know sometimes in the environment the
relationship is more with who? With
peers. Maybe it's not for the CEO all
the time. And we're like, "Whatever,
Frank." You're like, "No, Pat,
seriously. What the hell you want to do?
Your wife and kids are expect You can
make a lot Why are you always going to
this negative place? What are you
doing?"
Like, "No, you're right, man. I got to
work on it." And I was seriously like,
"When is this going to change?"
This never comes to me. It's handled by

are rolling up to you. Yes.

five-trillion-dollar companies in
Nvidia, that had a longer path to go.
So, be very careful jumping in on day
one. But, they say modest gains 28% make
a modest gain, and 12% are strong
winners. Remember, there's 40% that won
or that did well. People ask me, "Hey
BizDoc,
what would you do or what do you think
the path is?" If you were to go look at
any of a number articles on investing in
IPOs, you will probably hear that the
third, fourth, fifth day or the end of
the first week of trading when there's
been settling, is probably a time if
you're going to buy and hold to buy.
Why? Because for the next year, you're
going to hit the 6-month mark called the
lockup period that usually will expire
in about 6 months. How do you find out?
You can Google it or you can go to w
www.sec.gov
and look at the S-1, and it'll tell you
exactly. There's usually a lot of glut
that ends up on the market. What do I
mean? Well, there's a lot of shares that
are now unlocked and they're open to
trade on the market at 6 months, and
they are locked up before that to
prevent excess volume or supply on the
market, which will drop the price of the
stock. Typically, when you have excess
supply, prices drop. So, that's where it
is. So, if you bought at the end of that
and held through the lockup period to
the end, you'll find out where it's
going to go. Now, which ones do you buy
and which ones don't you buy? That's
between you and your broker and God.
But, typically, that is the methodology
that people put in place if they really
believe that those first three earnings
reports are going to be good and there's
a good forecast, they see what analysts
are saying about it, then get past all

purchase, decide you're going to buy and
hold, watch what happens at the lockup
period, you may see a sinking, but it
also is an opportunity when the lockup
period puts shares on and it goes down.
Usually there is, not always, but
usually then there is an uptick. What is
that? Well, that's demand coming in and
saying, "Hey, look at all those lockup
shares. It just dropped 10%. Maybe it's
a time to get in." Now the demand brings
it back up. So lockup periods, depending
on which stock, can go like this, they
can go like this, or they can go like
this.
But typically, the lockup is not a jump
up because it's not accompanied by good
news. "We just signed a deal with God
and he's going to buy 14 million of our
chips." No, it's usually just, "Hey,
fair warning, the lockup period's
expiring for this company. Shares can be
traded then. Expect a lot of volume to
hit the market. Expect price to be
depressed a little bit by that volume,
and then let's see what happens if
there's an appetite with other retail
investors to come in and say, 'Well,
now's my opportunity. I'm going to go
buy some of that.'" So people ask, "What
do you do?" I hope that analysis gives
you some thoughts about what you could
do. The number one thing, be smart, not
impulsive, and a patient investor,
because there are not many investors
that ever beat the S&P 500 over the
long, long haul. And so if you think
that overnight you're going to become

that. You can find a lot of Reddit

about this and that. You're going in and

## 30. Patrick Bet-David's Secret to Beating Bigger Competitors

When I was coming up, I worked with a
couple different guys. One of the guys
was in the business of bashing all the
competitors, and all they did is talk
negatively about competitors.
These guys suck. Those guys are
criminals. These guys are this. Those
guys are that.
And that was their model of talking
about how, you know, great they are. But
I'm a sports guy.
And when you watch sports, you'll hear
great coaches say you can never
underestimate
them in the playoffs. You can never
underestimate their defense. You can
never underestimate how champions show
up in the playoffs. So, you'll notice
the language of a sports coach
is different. They actually build up
their opponents. And And they know when
to build up the opponent and kind of
bring their team down and when to do the
opposite. And sometimes this is based on
it if you're the dominant team, if
you're the dominant team,
and you know you have a 10 time better
team than the other guys, you don't
build up your team. You do the opposite.
You actually build up the opponent.
Because you need your team to not get
cocky thinking because we're the
dominant team we're going to beat the
other guys. I I don't know if I'm making
sense. So, it's like
>> No, you're making a lot of sense.
>> Yeah, so sometimes, like the way I had
it is, you know, my guys that were, you
know,
kicking ass
and killing it with numbers, and they
were crushing it,
I would lift them up, but I would
typically humble them a little bit. I
would lift them up, but I would
privately be like, "You're doing good,
but you're not doing that great. I mean,
you're not there yet."
Right? Like when you think about guys
that do big things or kids that do big
things in their lives, they'll typically
complain about a father that said, well,
kind of like typically you'll see boys
that do so well in their lives, but
they'll go back and they'll say, "You
know what? You know, nothing was ever
enough for my dad."
Well, it's kind of it's a little bit of
leadership and driving.
Like what if it was you landed your
first $15 an hour job? Son, you're
freaking killing it in life. What if you
said something like that? So So you need
to kind of balance that out while you're
developing people and individuals. Like
if I have a if I have a one of my kids
that's super cocky, I bring them down. I
don't, you know, and I say you're not
really as good as you think you are. I
mean, I'm just going to look at that
guy. Like you're doing good, but you're
not there yet. Like we're doing okay.
And so that was the language for the
competition side. The other part is
is we would study the competition and I
would teach my guys the competition.
And I would tell them what areas we're
better than them. You know, I would very
openly talk to them. I would say, "Let
me tell you about what Powerhouse Gym
has over us. Bodybuilders will not come
to Bally Total Fitness. They're going to
go to Powerhouse to become members. You
go cuz Powerhouse has better steroids
than we do. We don't have steroids.
We're Bally Total Fitness. But if you
want steroids, you go to Powerhouse."
And I would say, you know, if you want
the pretty girls, it's probably going to
be Crunch cuz Crunch has better, you
know, what do you call it? Uh uh
I don't know. What do you call it? The
aerobic programs. But at Bally's, we
were the best all around and here's
where we win. Boom boom boom boom boom.
So that made my guys comfortable knowing
about the competition and I gave them
ammunition. When they sat with other
people, they knew what the edge was of
the opponent. So we didn't not talk
about it, but we talked about it that
way. But even with that, let me give you
the flip side of it.
At the end of the day, I would also

it is I." That I'm shaping that mindset.

as we're on on the way of doing

something that gets you out the door but
the second product is what makes you
famous. Be unafraid of giving up on
first products if you think a second
product is really going to be the one
that propels you. Don't pivot every 5
minutes and jitter jabber but really

to another and understand how number two
could become number one and you may be
launching X not as an X social media you

you have synergies of what you started
with. Many people on Instagram talk

the second or third product that they
came to offer. Whether you're offering
nutritional supplements crafts or other
things it's interesting how people say
don't be married to number one. Don't
pivot too quick and jump around but
definitely understand how number one can
be the path to number two, which makes a
super success out of you. And lastly, 1
+ 1 = 3. Sometimes you have the
opportunity that the first product
benefits from the second product, and
now they're sold together. That's what
we see with a Tesla. Tesla sells the

together where 1 + 1 can equal 3. 1 + 1
can equal 3 to you as well on a small
scale. This product plus this product,
boom, is something that now allows you
to sell both off into the future.

## 36. The 4 Things Every CEO Must Do to Grow a Company

I went to an event at Harvard for 3
weeks and I was with 144 other CEOs. I
was so afraid to go. Tom told me go. I
said Tom, there's no way I can be away
from the business for 3 weeks. He says,
you need to go. I went. I sat down next
to a guy, I don't know if you've heard
me tell the story or not. I sat down
next to a guy who runs the Victoria's
Secret of New Zealand and Australia.
He was worth 1.2, 1.3 billion at the
time. Extremely good looking guy.
Early 60s, late 50s, just suave and he's
you know, nails flawed, everything was
great. One of the two of us gave

them that gave the presentations in
front of 144. He got up there wanting to

models
wearing two-piece bikinis at Harvard.
Harvard got so nervous, they ran up to
them, started putting jackets around
these half-naked girls and he became the
fan favorite. Everybody loved this CEO.
This is the guy. So, just kind of
visualize who he is, that's him.
I sat next to him. I said, hey, so I'm
trying to be a better CEO, what do I do?
He says, well, I'm the chairman of the
board, I'm no longer a CEO. I said, how
many CEOs report to you? He says, I got
seven CEOs that report to me with 6,000
employees.
It was either seven CEO, 6,000 or six
CEOs and 7,000, one of those two.
I said, okay. I said, so how do you
become a better CEO? He says, being a
CEO is really four things you got to do.
Two things grow the company linear, two
things grow the company exponential. I
said, tell me. He says, if you want to
grow the company linear, 6 to 12%,
focus on improving your systems
and processes and technology and go out
there shaking hands, doing biz dev and
sales.
Network, meet people, tell people what
you're doing. All of a sudden you're

raise capital for you, but those are the

Systems, technology, biz dev, sales. I

have a very difficult time getting a
renewal or finding a carrier to cover
you. But nonetheless, understanding that
that risk in here means you need to be

what's going on." California has the
fair plan, which isn't particularly

## 58. Why Your Performance Reviews Are Costing You Millions ｜ Calibrations with RJ Milnor

Great companies are built from great
teams. When our people have a clear
direction and are aligned, they're not
only an accelerator of growth, they're a
multiplier of growth from our business.
But when there's noise, when they don't
know what to do and aren't aligned on
how to do it, the business growth
stalls. Over the next 5 minutes, I'm
going to show you a simple process
called calibration that removes the
noise. My name is RJ Milner. I'm the
Chief HR Officer from Bet David
Consulting. We found that about 70% of
all the variation in teams performance
comes down to just three things. Do they
understand the responsibilities of their
job? Do they have clear expectations of
how to do the job? And are they
receiving strong, specific feedback on
how to do that job? Now, most companies
try to address that problem with
performance evaluations. So, for
instance, you might be evaluating your
employees every year or every 6 months
or maybe every quarter like we do at Bet
David Consulting. But performance
evaluations alone are not sufficient.
And I'll show you why. Let's imagine
we're looking at the distribution of
ratings in your own performance
evaluations. So, perhaps you've got a
five-point scale like we do at Bet David
Consulting. Let's put that on the
x-axis. So, we could have five ratings.
One, two, three, four,
five, where one is the lowest
performance and five is the highest
performance.
And then on the y-axis, that'll just be
the frequency. So, how many of those
ratings in each category? Well, what
we'd expect from a performance
evaluation process, whether we're doing
it annually or quarterly, is some kind
of normal distribution that might look
like this.
So, most people are in the three
category in the middle, then there's an
even distribution of ratings on either
side.
Unfortunately, that's not what happens.
What happens in most organizations is
that the distribution curve looks like
this.
So, when managers submit their
evaluations, we find far fewer low
evaluations
and more that are slightly above average
or on the far right-hand side or high
side of the scale. That creates two
problems for us as a business and an
organization. The first is that the
people that really need constructive
feedback or development aren't getting
it. And the second is it can increase
our payroll expense. Let's take a look
at both of those really quickly. So,
look at this red line.
There are There are people in this spot
that are underperforming and not
contributing to the business and the and
the organization and also personally who
might actually thrive somewhere else in
other company. We're not identifying
them and they they might be someone that
we want to exit from the company and
could do better somewhere else.
There's also a larger group here
that could really use developmental
feedback. They might need opportunities
for improvement or development plans.
They're not getting that opportunity to
thrive. That hurts the business because
it slows our growth and it hurts them
individually because they're not getting
the opportunities they need. On the
other side of the scale, there's a
problem here, too. Cuz candidly, we're
telling people they're better than they
might actually be.
And in that situation, similar to over
here, they're not getting the feedback,
the development, and the expectations
that they need to drive performance and
to drive business growth and
performance.
That's one of the issues that we're
seeing.
The other though comes to actual expense
for the company.
If we're tying pay to performance, so
for instance,
paying the people at five and four more
than those at three, two, and one,
this significantly increases our payroll
expense
and maybe in a way that's not warranted
based upon the performance of the
workforce. So, how do we solve this
problem? The answer is a process called
calibration. And calibration creates
signal from all of this noise.
In the calibration process, we bring all
of our managers together along with the
executive team to review the ratings,
pressure test them, and finalize the
ratings that we want to share back to
the organization.
So, an example for us, we do our
performance evaluations every quarter.
So, at the end of that, on a quarterly
basis, we'll bring all of our managers
together along with the executive team
into a single meeting.
And during that meeting, we'll
understand first off, what is the
definition of each of these ratings,
what does it look like? We'll understand
what our top performers are doing, so we
have full transparency across all the
talent in our organization, so we can
compare people on an apples-to-apples
basis.
The other thing that we're doing is
we're inviting feedback from that entire
group, all the managers and executives,
on each person that's being reviewed.
So, as an example, if I'm reviewing my
talent, I might say, "This is what they
did over the quarter, this is the areas
where I think they might need
improvement, and this is the rating."
But I'm inviting feedback from others to
support that evaluation or to challenge
it. So, someone might tell me a story
that's supporting that evaluation about
something great the employee did, or
they might disagree with me. And I'm
inviting that disagreement and maybe a
change in the person's rating.
That philosophy is so important to
calibration, and it's a philosophy that
we call constructive conflict.
The purpose of the meeting is to have
those hard conversations, to make those
hard decisions, so that we're getting
the best possible feedback to the
employee and the best possible results
to the company. After calibration, we
have a finalized distribution of
performance ratings that we know we can
rely on. It's been pressure tested by
every single manager in the company and
by the executive team. It has the full
faith and endorsement not just of one
manager, where there could be bias or
bad judgment. Every single manager has
signed off on this, and they can be
delivered with confidence back to the
employee.
Now we can move from that red curve back
to the white curve, giving employees the
feedback that they need to develop,
while also doing the responsible thing
for the company, allowing the company to
both drive its performance forward and
accelerate, while also being responsible
with the money that we're spending on
bonuses or salary. What the calibration
process does is it removes noise from
the system. It allows the company to
operate faster, more directly, and give
employees those clear expectations and
feedback that they need to perform at
the highest level, and for the company
to perform at the highest level.
Are you interested in learning more? If
so, scan the QR code at the bottom, and
our Bet-David Consulting team will share
how we can help with calibration, or you
can even schedule a one-on-one with me
to help build your calibration process.
